    class Tool {
        +name: string
        +aliases: string[]
        +searchHint: string
        +inputSchema: ZodType
        +inputJSONSchema?: JSONSchema
        +maxResultSizeChars: number
        +call(): Promise~ToolResult~
        +description(): Promise~string~
        +checkPermissions(): Promise~PermissionResult~

        +isEnabled(): boolean
        +isReadOnly(input): boolean
        +isDestructive?(input): boolean

        +validateInput?(): Promise~ValidationResult~
    }

        +data: T

aa:["$","h2",null,{"children":"Complete Tool Execution Flow"}]
ab:["$","p",null,{"children":"Integrating all the layers above, here is the complete flow from when a tool is called by the AI to when execution completes:"}]
e4:T59f,sequenceDiagram
    participant AI as LLM Response
    participant Q as query() loop
    participant STE as StreamingToolExecutor
    participant Perm as Permission System
    participant Tool as Concrete Tool

    AI->>Q: tool_use: { name: "Bash", input: { command: "npm test" } }
    Q->>STE: addTool(toolUseBlock, assistantMessage)

    STE->>STE: findToolByName() look up tool definition
    STE->>STE: inputSchema.safeParse() + isConcurrencySafe()

    Note over STE: canExecuteTool() checks concurrency conditions

    STE->>Perm: runToolUse() -> canUseTool()
    Perm->>Perm: Layer 1: runHooks()
    Perm->>Perm: Layer 2: tryClassifier()
    Perm->>Perm: Layer 3-4: deny/allow rule matching
    Perm->>Perm: Layer 5: Permission mode check
    alt Requires user confirmation
        Perm->>Perm: Layer 6: queueOps.push() show dialog
    end

    alt Allow
        Perm-->>STE: PermissionAllowDecision
        STE->>Tool: call(args, context, canUseTool, parentMessage, onProgress)
        Tool->>Tool: Execute actual operation
        loop Progress updates
            Tool-->>STE: onProgress(progressMessage)
            STE-->>Q: Yield progress message immediately
        end
        Tool-->>STE: ToolResult { data, newMessages? }
        STE-->>Q: Yield result message (in order)
    else Deny
        Perm-->>STE: PermissionDenyDecision
        STE-->>Q: Yield denial message
        Q->>AI: Inform AI the tool was denied
    endac:["$","$L17",null,{"chart":"$e4"}]

d2:["$","h3",null,{"children":"1. Declarative Tool Behavior Design"}]
d3:["$","p",null,{"children":["Having tools self-declare their behavioral characteristics through methods like ",["$","code",null,{"className":"rounded bg-[#1a1a2e] px-1.5 py-0.5 text-[0.875em] text-[#e0c4a8] before:content-none after:content-none","children":"isConcurrencySafe()"}],", ",["$","code",null,{"className":"rounded bg-[#1a1a2e] px-1.5 py-0.5 text-[0.875em] text-[#e0c4a8] before:content-none after:content-none","children":"interruptBehavior()"}],", and ",["$","code",null,{"className":"rounded bg-[#1a1a2e] px-1.5 py-0.5 text-[0.875em] text-[#e0c4a8] before:content-none after:content-none","children":"isReadOnly()"}],", rather than the scheduler hard-coding each tool's behavior. This lets new tools seamlessly plug into the scheduling system without modifying the scheduler code. StreamingToolExecutor's ",["$","code",null,{"className":"rounded bg-[#1a1a2e] px-1.5 py-0.5 text-[0.875em] text-[#e0c4a8] before:content-none after:content-none","children":"canExecuteTool()"}]," method is only 6 lines, yet it correctly handles any number of tool combinations — because the scheduling logic is entirely based on tools' self-declarations."]}]
d4:["$","h3",null,{"children":"2. Dual-Track Schema"}]
d5:["$","p",null,{"children":["When a system needs to support both internal definitions and external protocols simultaneously, providing two schema formats (Zod + JSON Schema) is a pragmatic choice. The key is unifying the processing logic at runtime — ",["$","code",null,{"className":"rounded bg-[#1a1a2e] px-1.5 py-0.5 text-[0.875em] text-[#e0c4a8] before:content-none after:content-none","children":"safeParse()"}]," is used for built-in tool input validation, while MCP tools' JSON Schema is used directly during API serialization. Callers don't need to care about the schema's source format."]}]
d6:["$","h3",null,{"children":"3. Layered Permissions with Source Tracking"}]
d7:["$","p",null,{"children":["The approach of recording rule sources (user/project/policy) in the permission system is worth emulating. It not only makes conflict resolution traceable, but also enables audit trails — administrators can see which permissions come from organizational policy and which were configured by users. ",["$","code",null,{"className":"rounded bg-[#1a1a2e] px-1.5 py-0.5 text-[0.875em] text-[#e0c4a8] before:content-none after:content-none","children":"filterToolsByDenyRules()"}]," filters out unavailable tools at the tool pool assembly stage, preventing the AI from wasting tokens trying to call a tool that will always be denied."]}]
d8:["$","h3",null,{"children":"4. Immutable Permission Context"}]
d9:["$","p",null,{"children":["The ",["$","code",null,{"className":"rounded bg-[#1a1a2e] px-1.5 py-0.5 text-[0.875em] text-[#e0c4a8] before:content-none after:content-none","children":"DeepImmutable<ToolPermissionContext>"}]," design ensures consistency in permission evaluation. In any system requiring security-critical decisions, the immutability of the decision context is an effective defense against TOCTOU (Time-of-check to time-of-use) vulnerabilities. Creating new objects rather than modifying existing ones when updating permissions is a pattern also widely used in React's state management."]}]
da:["$","h3",null,{"children":"5. Selective Propagation of Cascading Cancellation"}]
db:["$","p",null,{"children":["In ",["$","code",null,{"className":"rounded bg-[#1a1a2e] px-1.5 py-0.5 text-[0.875em] text-[#e0c4a8] before:content-none after:content-none","children":"StreamingToolExecutor"}],", only BashTool errors trigger sibling abort, not errors from all tools. This \"selective cascade\" is more precise than \"cancel all\" or \"cancel none\" — it makes judgments based on domain knowledge about inter-tool dependencies. This pattern can be generalized to any scenario requiring concurrent cancellation."]}]
dc:["$","h3",null,{"children":"6. Race-Safe Decision Resolution"}]
dd:["$","p",null,{"children":["The ",["$","code",null,{"className":"rounded bg-[#1a1a2e] px-1.5 py-0.5 text-[0.875em] text-[#e0c4a8] before:content-none after:content-none","children":"ResolveOnce"}]," ",["$","code",null,{"className":"rounded bg-[#1a1a2e] px-1.5 py-0.5 text-[0.875em] text-[#e0c4a8] before:content-none after:content-none","children":"claim()"}]," pattern provides an elegant solution for async race conditions. In permission dialogs, user actions and system cancellations can arrive simultaneously — ",["$","code",null,{"className":"rounded bg-[#1a1a2e] px-1.5 py-0.5 text-[0.875em] text-[#e0c4a8] before:content-none after:content-none","children":"claim()"}]," ensures only one callback \"wins\" the decision authority, avoiding duplicate resolves and unpredictable side effects."]}]
